season">2001</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2002_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2002 New York Yankees season">2002</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2003_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2003 New York Yankees season">2012</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Wild card berths <span style="font-weight:normal;">(7)</span></th><td>\n<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/2007_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2007 New York Yankees season">2007</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2010_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2010 New York Yankees season">2010</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2015_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2015 New York Yankees season">2015</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2017_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2017 New York Yankees season">2017</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/2018_New_York_Yankees_season" title="2018 New York Yankees season">2018</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #dcdcdc">Front office</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Owner(s)</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Yankee_Global_Enterprises" title="Yankee Global Enterprises">Yankee Global Enterprises</a><br />(<a href="/wiki/Hal_Steinbrenner" title="Hal Steinbrenner">Hal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hank_Steinbrenner" title="Hank Steinbrenner">Hank Steinbrenner</a>, co-chairmen)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Aaron_Boone" title="Aaron Boone">Aaron Boone</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">General Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Brian_Cashman" title="Brian Cashman">Brian Cashman</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">President of Baseball Operations</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Randy_Levine" title="Randy Levine">Randy Levine</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>New York Yankees</b> are an American professional <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> team based in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> <a href="/wiki/Borough_(New_York_City)" class="mw-redirect" title="Borough (New York City)">borough</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">the Bronx</a>. The Yankees compete in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) as a member club of the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) <a href="/wiki/American_League_East" title="American League East">East division</a>. They are one of two major league clubs based in New York City, the other being the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/1901_in_baseball" title="1901 in baseball">1901</a> season, the club began play in the AL as the <b>Baltimore Orioles</b> (no relation to the modern <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a>). <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Farrell" title="Frank J. Farrell">Frank Farrell</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Stephen_Devery" title="William Stephen Devery">Bill Devery</a> purchased the franchise (which had ceased operations) and moved it to New York City, renaming the club the <b>New York Highlanders</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-namechanges_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-namechanges-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The Highlanders were officially renamed the <b>Yankees</b> in <a href="/wiki/1913_in_baseball" title="1913 in baseball">1913</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-namechanges_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-namechanges-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The team is owned by <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Global_Enterprises" title="Yankee Global Enterprises">Yankee Global Enterprises</a>, an LLC controlled by the family of the late <a href="/wiki/George_Steinbrenner" title="George Steinbrenner">George Steinbrenner</a>, who purchased the team in 1973. <a href="/wiki/Brian_Cashman" title="Brian Cashman">Brian Cashman</a> is the team\'s general manager, and <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Boone" title="Aaron Boone">Aaron Boone</a> is the team\'s <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">field manager</a>. The team\'s home games were played at the <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">original Yankee Stadium</a> from 1923 to 1973 and from 1976 to 2008. In 1974 and 1975, the Yankees shared <a href="/wiki/Shea_Stadium" title="Shea Stadium">Shea Stadium</a> with the Mets, in addition to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Jets" title="New York Jets">New York Jets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_Giants" title="New York Giants">New York Giants</a>. In 2009, they moved into a <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium" title="Yankee Stadium">new ballpark of the same name</a> after the previous facility was closed and demolished.<sup id="cite_ref-newstadiumopening_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newstadiumopening-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The team is perennially among the leaders in <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_attendance_figures" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Major League Baseball attendance figures">MLB attendance</a>; in 2011, the Yankees had the second-highest attendance.\n</p><p>As one of the most successful sports clubs in the world, the Yankees have won 18 division titles, 40 <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_League_pennant_winners" title="List of American League pennant winners">AL pennants</a>, and 27 <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Series_champions" title="List of World Series champions">World Series championships</a>, all of which are MLB records.<sup id="cite_ref-win25_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-win25-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Revenue_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revenue-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The Yankees have won more titles than any other franchise in the four major North American sports leagues. Forty-four Yankees players and eleven Yankees managers have been inducted into the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame">National Baseball Hall of Fame</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yogi_Berra" title="Yogi Berra">Yogi Berra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Whitey_Ford" title="Whitey Ford">Whitey Ford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In pursuit of winning championships, the franchise has used a large payroll to attract talent, particularly during the Steinbrenner era. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i>, the Yankees are the second <a href="/wiki/Forbes%27_list_of_the_most_valuable_sports_teams" title="Forbes&#39; list of the most valuable sports teams">highest valued sports franchise</a> in the United States and the fifth in the world, with an estimated value of approximately $4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Yankees have garnered enormous popularity and a dedicated fanbase, as well as widespread enmity from fans of other MLB teams. The team\'s <a href="/wiki/Yankees%E2%80%93Red_Sox_rivalry" title="Yankees\xe2\x80\x93Red Sox rivalry">rivalry</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> is one of the most well-known rivalries in U.S. sports.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(December 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_New_York_Yankees" title="History of the New York Yankees">History of the New York Yankees</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Origins_in_Baltimore_.281901.E2.80.931902.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_in_Baltimore_(1901\xe2\x80\x931902)">Origins in Baltimore (1901\xe2\x80\x931902) <span id="Baltimore"></span></span></h3>\n<p>At the end of 1900, <a href="/wiki/Ban_Johnson" title="Ban Johnson">Ban Johnson</a>, president of the <a href="/wiki/Western_League_(original)" class="mw-redirect" title="Western League (original)">American League</a>\xe2\x80\x94a <a href="/wiki/Minor_League_Baseball" title="Minor League Baseball">minor league</a> previously known as the Western League (1894\xe2\x80\x931899)\xe2\x80\x94reorganized the league. He added teams in three East Coast cities, forming the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) as a major league in an attempt to challenge the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) for supremacy. Plans to add a team in New York City were blocked by the NL\'s <a href="/wiki/New_York_Giants_(NL)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a>, who had enough political power in New York City to prevent the AL from establishing a team. Instead, a team was placed in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, Maryland, a city which the NL abandoned when it contracted from 12 to 8 teams in 1900.\n</p><p>Nicknamed the Orioles, the team began playing in 1901 and was managed and partly owned by <a href="/wiki/John_McGraw_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="John McGraw (baseball)">John McGraw</a>. During the 1902 season, McGraw feuded with Johnson and secretly jumped to the Giants. In the middle of the season, the Giants, aided and abetted by McGraw, gained controlling interest of the Orioles and began raiding it for players, until the AL stepped in and took control of the team. In January 1903, a "peace conference" was held between the two leagues to settle disputes and try to coexist. At the conference, Johnson requested that an AL team be put in New York, to play alongside the NL\'s Giants.<sup id="cite_ref-Secyclo_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secyclo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It was put to a vote, and 15 of the 16 major league owners agreed on it, with only <a href="/wiki/John_T._Brush" title="John T. Brush">John T. Brush</a> of the Giants opposing. The Orioles\' new owners, <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Farrell" title="Frank J. Farrell">Frank J. Farrell</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_S._Devery" class="mw-redirect" title="William S. Devery">William S. Devery</a>, found a ballpark location not blocked by the Giants, and Baltimore\'s team moved to New York.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Move_to_New_York:_the_Highlanders_years_.281903.E2.80.931912.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Move_to_New_York:_the_Highlanders_years_(1903\xe2\x80\x931912)">Move to New York: the Highlanders years (1903\xe2\x80\x931912)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hilltop4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Hilltop4.jpg/220px-Hilltop4.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Hilltop4.jpg/330px-Hilltop4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Hilltop4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hilltop4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hilltop_Park" title="Hilltop Park">Hilltop Park</a>, home of the Highlanders</div></div></div>\n<p>The team\'s new ballpark, <a href="/wiki/Hilltop_Park" title="Hilltop Park">Hilltop Park</a> (formally known as "American League Park"), was constructed in one of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">Upper Manhattan</a>\'s highest points\xe2\x80\x94between 165th and 168th Streets\xe2\x80\x94just a few blocks away from the much larger <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a>. The team came to be known as the New York Highlanders. The name was inspired by a combination of the team\'s elevated location in Upper Manhattan, and to the noted Scottish military unit <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Highlanders" title="Gordon Highlanders">The Gordon Highlanders</a>, which coincided with the team\'s president Joseph Gordon whose family was of <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_people" title="Ulster Scots people">Scots Irish</a> heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Newspapers initially called the team "Gordon\'s Highlanders" (e.g. New York <i>World</i>, April 15, 1903), which soon became just "Highlanders". As was common with all members of the American League, the team was often called the New York Americans. They were also dubbed the "Invaders" for a short time in 1903. <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Press_(historical)" title="New York Press (historical)">New York Press</a></i> Sports Editor Jim Price coined the unofficial nickname Yankees (or "Yanks") for the club as early as 1904, because it was easier to fit in headlines and because "Yankee" was and is a commonly-used synonym for "American".<sup id="cite_ref-Secyclo_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secyclo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A prophetic letter to the editor of the New York <i>Sun</i>, May 7, 1903, p.&#160;8, had raised this question: "<b>Name for the American New Yorks.</b> If the new baseball team is to have a name that is in keeping with the \'Giants,\' does it not seem reasonable that if they are the \'New York Americans\' they might be called the \'Yankees\' or \'Yanks\'?"\n</p><p>The most success the Highlanders achieved was finishing second in 1904, 1906 and 1910, 1904 being the closest they came to winning the AL pennant. That year, they lost the deciding game on the last day of the season to the <a href="/wiki/1904_Boston_Americans_season" title="1904 Boston Americans season">Boston Americans</a>, who later became the Boston Red Sox. This had much historical significance, as the Highlanders\' role in the pennant race caused the Giants to announce that they would not play in the <a href="/wiki/1904_World_Series" title="1904 World Series">World Series</a> against the AL pennant winner. The World Series was not skipped again for another 90 years, when a <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1994\xe2\x80\x9395 Major League Baseball strike">strike</a> truncated the 1994 season. It was the last time Boston beat New York in a pennant-deciding game for a full century (2004). In 1904, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Jack_Chesbro" title="Jack Chesbro">Jack Chesbro</a> set the single-season wins record at 41, which still stands. Under current playing practices, this is most likely an unbreakable record, especially considering that the last thirty game winner was <a href="/wiki/Denny_McLain" title="Denny McLain">Denny McLain</a> (31) in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="New_owners.2C_a_new_home.2C_and_a_new_name:_Years_at_the_Polo_Grounds_.281913.E2.80.931922.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_owners,_a_new_home,_and_a_new_name:_Years_at_the_Polo_Grounds_(1913\xe2\x80\x931922)">New owners, a new home, and a new name: Years at the Polo Grounds (1913\xe2\x80\x931922)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polo_Grounds_after_1911.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Polo_Grounds_after_1911.JPG/220px-Polo_Grounds_after_1911.JPG" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Polo_Grounds_after_1911.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="315" data-file-height="208" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polo_Grounds_after_1911.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a>, home of the Yankees from 1913 to 1922</div></div></div>\n<p>The original <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a> burned down in 1911 and the Highlanders allowed the Giants to play in Hilltop Park during reconstruction. Relations between the two teams warmed, and the Highlanders moved into the newly rebuilt Polo Grounds in 1913. Playing on the Harlem River, a far cry from their high-altitude home, the name "Highlanders" no longer applied, and fell into disuse among the press. The media had already widely adopted the "Yankees" nickname coined by the <i>New York Press</i>, and in 1913 the team became officially known as the New York Yankees.\n</p><p>By the middle of the decade, Yankees owners Farrell and Devery had become estranged and both were in dire need of money. At the start of 1915, they sold the team to Colonel <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Ruppert" title="Jacob Ruppert">Jacob Ruppert</a> and Captain <a href="/wiki/Tillinghast_L%27Hommedieu_Huston" title="Tillinghast L&#39;Hommedieu Huston">Tillinghast L\'Hommedieu Huston</a> for $1.25 million.<sup id="cite_ref-namechanges_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-namechanges-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Ruppert inherited a brewery fortune, providing the Yankees with an owner who possessed deep pockets and a willingness to dig into them to produce a winning team. This led the team to more success and prestige than Ruppert could ever have envisioned.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>All the games of the 1921 and 1922 World Series were played in the Polo Grounds, when the Yankees squared off against their intracity rival Giants.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Sluggers_and_the_Stadium:_Ruth.2C_Gehrig.2C_and_Murderer.27s_Row_.281923.E2.80.931935.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sluggers_and_the_Stadium:_Ruth,_Gehrig,_and_Murderer\'s_Row_(1923\xe2\x80\x931935)">Sluggers and the Stadium: Ruth, Gehrig, and Murderer\'s Row (1923\xe2\x80\x931935)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Babe_Ruth2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Babe_Ruth2.jpg/170px-Babe_Ruth2.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Babe_Ruth2.jpg/255px-Babe_Ruth2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Babe_Ruth2.jpg/340px-Babe_Ruth2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2623" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Babe_Ruth2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>With his hitting prowess, <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> ushered in an offensive-oriented era of baseball and helped lead the Yankees to four World Series titles.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the years around 1920, the Yankees, the Red Sox, and the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a> had a <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="D\xc3\xa9tente">d\xc3\xa9tente</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The trades between the three ballclubs antagonized Ban Johnson and garnered the teams the nickname "The Insurrectos".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StoutArticle_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StoutArticle-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This d\xc3\xa9tente paid off well for the Yankees as they increased their payroll. Most new players who later contributed to the team\'s success came from the Red Sox, whose owner, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Frazee" title="Harry Frazee">Harry Frazee</a>, was trading them for large sums of money to finance his theatrical productions.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Pitcher-turned-outfielder <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> was the most talented of all the acquisitions from Boston, and the outcome of that trade haunted the Red Sox <a href="/wiki/2004_World_Series" title="2004 World Series">for the next 86 years</a>, a span in which the team did not win a single World Series championship. The Red Sox often found themselves eliminated from the playoff hunt as a result of the Yankees\' success.<sup id="cite_ref-FiercestRivalry_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FiercestRivalry-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> This phenomenon eventually became known as the <a href="/wiki/Curse_of_the_Bambino" title="Curse of the Bambino">Curse of the Bambino</a> as the failure of the Red Sox and the success of the Yankees seemed almost supernatural, and seemed to stem from that one trade. However, it was not until 1990 when <a href="/wiki/Dan_Shaughnessy" title="Dan Shaughnessy">Dan Shaughnessy</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i> wrote a book with the same title that the curse was publicized.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ruth\'s multitude of home runs proved so popular that the Yankees began drawing more people than their National League counterpart, the Giants.<sup id="cite_ref-StoutArticle_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StoutArticle-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1921\xe2\x80\x94the year after acquiring Babe Ruth\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/1921_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1921 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> played in their <a href="/wiki/1921_World_Series" title="1921 World Series">first World Series</a>. They competed against the <a href="/wiki/1921_New_York_Giants_season" title="1921 New York Giants season">Giants</a>, and all eight games of the series were played in the Polo Grounds. After the 1922 season, the Yankees were told to move out of the Polo Grounds. Giants manager <a href="/wiki/John_McGraw" title="John McGraw">John McGraw</a> was said to have commented that the Yankees should "move to some out-of-the-way place, like Queens", but they instead broke ground for a new ballpark in <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">the Bronx</a>, right across the <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">Harlem River</a> from the Polo Grounds. In 1922, the <a href="/wiki/1922_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1922 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> returned to the <a href="/wiki/1922_World_Series" title="1922 World Series">World Series</a> again, and were dealt a second defeat at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/1922_New_York_Giants_season" title="1922 New York Giants season">Giants</a>. Important newcomers in this period were manager <a href="/wiki/Miller_Huggins" title="Miller Huggins">Miller Huggins</a> and general manager <a href="/wiki/Ed_Barrow" title="Ed Barrow">Ed Barrow</a>. The hiring of Huggins by Ruppert in 1918 caused a rift between the owners that eventually led to Ruppert buying Huston out in 1923.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gehrig_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Gehrig_cropped.jpg/170px-Gehrig_cropped.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Gehrig_cropped.jpg/255px-Gehrig_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Gehrig_cropped.jpg/340px-Gehrig_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1371" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gehrig_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a> was the first Yankees player to have his number retired, in 1939.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1923, the Yankees moved to their new home, <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a>. It was the first triple-deck venue in baseball and seated an astounding 58,000 people. In the first game at Yankee Stadium, Babe Ruth hit a home run, which was fitting as his home runs and drawing power paid for the stadium, giving it its nickname of "The House That Ruth Built".<sup id="cite_ref-Secyclo_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secyclo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the season, the <a href="/wiki/1923_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1923 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> faced the <a href="/wiki/1923_New_York_Giants_season" title="1923 New York Giants season">Giants</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1923_World_Series" title="1923 World Series">World Series</a> for the third straight year, and triumphed in the brand new ballpark for their first championship. Prior to that point, the Giants had been the city\'s icon and dominant team. From 1923 onward, the Yankees assumed that role, and many years later the Giants left <a href="/wiki/Coogan%27s_Bluff" title="Coogan&#39;s Bluff">Coogan\'s Bluff</a> for San Francisco.\n</p><p>In the 1927 <a href="/wiki/1927_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1927 Major League Baseball season">season</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1927_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1927 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> featured a lineup that became known as "<a href="/wiki/Murderers%27_Row" title="Murderers&#39; Row">Murderers\' Row</a>", and some consider this team to be the best in the history of baseball (though similar claims have been made for other Yankee squads, notably those of 1939, 1961 and 1998).<sup id="cite_ref-1927best_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1927best-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The Yankees won a then-AL record 110 games with only 44 losses, and swept the <a href="/wiki/1927_Pittsburgh_Pirates_season" title="1927 Pittsburgh Pirates season">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1927_World_Series" title="1927 World Series">World Series</a>. Ruth\'s home run total of 60 in 1927 set a single-season home run record that stood until it was broken by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Maris" title="Roger Maris">Roger Maris</a> in 1961. Meanwhile, first baseman <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a> had his first big season, batting .373 with 47 home runs and 175 RBIs, beating Ruth\'s single-season RBI mark (171 in 1921). The Yankees won the World Series again in 1928.\n</p><p>In 1931, <a href="/wiki/Joe_McCarthy_(manager)" title="Joe McCarthy (manager)">Joe McCarthy</a> was hired as manager and brought the <a href="/wiki/1931_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1931 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> back to the top of the AL. They swept the <a href="/wiki/1932_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1932 Chicago Cubs season">Chicago Cubs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1932_World_Series" title="1932 World Series">1932 World Series</a>, and brought the team\'s streak of consecutive World Series game wins to 12. This series was made famous by Babe Ruth\'s "<a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth%27s_Called_Shot" class="mw-redirect" title="Babe Ruth&#39;s Called Shot">Called Shot</a>" in game three of the series at <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a>, a fitting "swan song" to his illustrious World Series career.  In 1935, Ruth left the Yankees to join the NL\'s <a href="/wiki/Boston_Braves_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Braves (baseball)">Boston Braves</a>, and he made his last major league baseball appearance on May 30 of that year.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Joltin.27_Joe_DiMaggio_.281936.E2.80.931951.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Joltin\'_Joe_DiMaggio_(1936\xe2\x80\x931951)">Joltin\' Joe DiMaggio (1936\xe2\x80\x931951)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DiMaggio_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/DiMaggio_cropped.jpg/180px-DiMaggio_cropped.jpg" width="180" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/DiMaggio_cropped.jpg/270px-DiMaggio_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/DiMaggio_cropped.jpg/360px-DiMaggio_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="563" data-file-height="868" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DiMaggio_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1941, <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a> set an MLB record with a 56-game hitting streak that stands to this day and will probably never be broken.</div></div></div>\n<p>With Ruth retired, Gehrig finally had a chance to take center stage, but it was only one year before a new star appeared, <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>. The team won an unprecedented four straight World Series titles from 1936 to 1939. For most of 1939, however, they had to do it without Gehrig, who took himself out of the lineup and retired due to <a href="/wiki/Amyotrophic_lateral_sclerosis" title="Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis">amyotrophic lateral sclerosis</a>, which is commonly known as "Lou Gehrig\'s Disease" in his memory. The Yankees declared July 4, 1939 to be "Lou Gehrig Day", on which they retired his number 4, the first retired number in baseball. Gehrig made a famous speech in which he declared himself to be "the luckiest man on the face of the earth."<sup id="cite_ref-GehrigSpeech_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GehrigSpeech-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He died two years later.\n</p><p>The 1941 baseball season was often described as the last year of the "Golden Era" before World War II and other realities intervened. It was a thrilling year as America watched two major events unfold: <a href="/wiki/Ted_Williams" title="Ted Williams">Ted Williams</a> of the Red Sox hitting for the elusive .400 batting average and Joe DiMaggio getting hits in consecutive ballgames. By the end of his hitting streak, DiMaggio hit in 56 consecutive games, the current major league record and one often deemed unbreakable.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two months after the Yankees beat the <a href="/wiki/1941_Brooklyn_Dodgers_season" title="1941 Brooklyn Dodgers season">Brooklyn Dodgers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1941_World_Series" title="1941 World Series">1941 World Series</a> -- the first of seven October meetings between the two crosstown rivals before the Dodgers ultimately moved to California -- the United States was attacked at <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> by the Empire of Japan, and many of their best players, including DiMaggio, were <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_States" title="Conscription in the United States">drafted</a> into the military to fight in World War II. The Yankees still managed to pull out a win against the <a href="/wiki/1943_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1943 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis Cardinals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1943_World_Series" title="1943 World Series">1943 World Series</a>.\n</p><p>In 1945 construction magnate <a href="/wiki/Del_Webb" title="Del Webb">Del Webb</a> and partners <a href="/wiki/Dan_Topping" title="Dan Topping">Dan Topping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larry_MacPhail" title="Larry MacPhail">Larry MacPhail</a> purchased the team from the Ruppert estate for $2.8 million; MacPhail was bought out in 1947.\n</p><p>After a few slumping seasons, McCarthy was fired early in 1946. A few interim managers later, <a href="/wiki/Bucky_Harris" title="Bucky Harris">Bucky Harris</a> took the job, righting the ship and taking the Yankees to a hard fought <a href="/wiki/1947_World_Series" title="1947 World Series">series</a> victory against the <a href="/wiki/1947_Brooklyn_Dodgers_season" title="1947 Brooklyn Dodgers season">Dodgers</a>.\n</p><p>Despite finishing only three games behind the <a href="/wiki/1948_Cleveland_Indians_season" title="1948 Cleveland Indians season">Cleveland Indians</a> in the 1948 pennant race, Harris was relieved of his duties and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Casey_Stengel" title="Casey Stengel">Casey Stengel</a>, who had a reputation of being a clown and managing bad teams. His tenure as Yankee field manager, however, was marked with success. The "underdog" Yankees came from behind to catch and surprise a powerful <a href="/wiki/1949_Boston_Red_Sox_season" title="1949 Boston Red Sox season">Red Sox</a> team on the last two days of the 1949 season, a face off that fueled the beginning of the modern <a href="/wiki/Yankees%E2%80%93Red_Sox_rivalry" title="Yankees\xe2\x80\x93Red Sox rivalry">Yankees\xe2\x80\x93Red Sox rivalry</a>. By this time, however, DiMaggio\'s career was winding down, and the "Yankee Clipper" retired after the 1951 season. This year marked the arrival of the "Oklahoma Kid", <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a>, who was one of several new stars who filled the gap.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Stengel.27s_squad_in_the_1950s_.281951.E2.80.931959.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stengel\'s_squad_in_the_1950s_(1951\xe2\x80\x931959)">Stengel\'s squad in the 1950s (1951\xe2\x80\x931959)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OpeningDay1951.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/OpeningDay1951.gif/220px-OpeningDay1951.gif" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/OpeningDay1951.gif/330px-OpeningDay1951.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/OpeningDay1951.gif/440px-OpeningDay1951.gif 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OpeningDay1951.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Opening_Day" title="Opening Day">Opening Day</a> of the 1951 baseball season at <a href="/wiki/Griffith_Stadium" title="Griffith Stadium">Griffith Stadium</a>. President <a href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> throws out the first ball as <a href="/wiki/Casey_Stengel" title="Casey Stengel">Casey Stengel</a> looks on.</div></div></div>\n<p>Bettering the clubs managed by Joe McCarthy, the Yankees won the World Series five consecutive times from <a href="/wiki/1949_World_Series" title="1949 World Series">1949</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/1953_World_Series" title="1953 World Series">1953</a> under Stengel, which continues to be the major league record. Led by players like center fielder <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a>, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Whitey_Ford" title="Whitey Ford">Whitey Ford</a>, and catcher <a href="/wiki/Yogi_Berra" title="Yogi Berra">Yogi Berra</a>, Stengel\'s teams won ten pennants and seven World Series titles in his twelve seasons as the Yankees manager. Stengel was a master at publicity for the team and for himself, even landing a cover story in <i>Time</i> magazine in 1955. The <a href="/wiki/1950_World_Series" title="1950 World Series">1950</a> title was the only one of those five championships not to be won against either the <a href="/wiki/Dodgers%E2%80%93Giants_rivalry" title="Dodgers\xe2\x80\x93Giants rivalry">New York Giants or Brooklyn Dodgers</a>; it was won in four straight games against the <a href="/wiki/Whiz_Kids_(baseball)" title="Whiz Kids (baseball)">Whiz Kids</a> of the <a href="/wiki/1950_Philadelphia_Phillies_season" title="1950 Philadelphia Phillies season">Philadelphia Phillies</a>.\n</p><p>In 1954, the <a href="/wiki/1954_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1954 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> won over 100 games, but the <a href="/wiki/1954_Cleveland_Indians_season" title="1954 Cleveland Indians season">Indians</a> took the pennant with an AL record 111 wins; 1954 was famously referred to as <i>The Year the Yankees Lost the Pennant</i>. In <a href="/wiki/1955_World_Series" title="1955 World Series">1955</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1955_Brooklyn_Dodgers_season" title="1955 Brooklyn Dodgers season">Dodgers</a> finally beat the <a href="/wiki/1955_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1955 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1955_World_Series" title="1955 World Series">World Series</a>, after five previous Series losses to them, but the Yankees came back strong the next year. On October 8, 1956, in Game Five of the <a href="/wiki/1956_World_Series" title="1956 World Series">1956 World Series</a> against the <a href="/wiki/1956_Brooklyn_Dodgers_season" title="1956 Brooklyn Dodgers season">Dodgers</a>, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Don_Larsen" title="Don Larsen">Don Larsen</a> threw the only <a href="/wiki/Perfect_game" title="Perfect game">perfect game</a> in World Series history,<sup id="cite_ref-PerfectLarsen_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PerfectLarsen-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> which remains the only perfect game in postseason play and was the only <a href="/wiki/No-hitter" title="No-hitter">no-hitter</a> of any kind to be pitched in postseason play until <a href="/wiki/Roy_Halladay" title="Roy Halladay">Roy Halladay</a> pitched a no-hitter on October 6, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1957_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1957 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> lost the <a href="/wiki/1957_World_Series" title="1957 World Series">1957 World Series</a> to the <a href="/wiki/1957_Milwaukee_Braves_season" title="1957 Milwaukee Braves season">Milwaukee Braves</a> when <a href="/wiki/Lew_Burdette" title="Lew Burdette">Lew Burdette</a> incredibly won three games for the Braves. Following the Series, the New York Giants and the Brooklyn Dodgers both left for California, the former leaving for San Francisco and the latter moving to Los Angeles, leaving the Yankees as New York\'s only baseball team. In the <a href="/wiki/1958_World_Series" title="1958 World Series">1958 World Series</a>, the Yankees got their revenge against the Braves, and became the second team to win the Series after being down three-games-to-one. For the decade, the Yankees won six World Series championships (\'50, 51, \'52, \'53, \'56, \'58) and eight American League pennants (those six plus \'55 and \'57). Led by Mantle, Ford, Berra, <a href="/wiki/Elston_Howard" title="Elston Howard">Elston Howard</a> (the Yankees\' first African-American player), and the newly acquired <a href="/wiki/Roger_Maris" title="Roger Maris">Roger Maris</a>, the Yankees entered the 1960s seeking to replicate their success of the 1950s.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_M.26M_Boys:_Mantle_and_Maris_.281960.E2.80.931964.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_M&amp;M_Boys:_Mantle_and_Maris_(1960\xe2\x80\x931964)">The M&amp;M Boys: Mantle and Maris (1960\xe2\x80\x931964)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg/170px-Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg" width="170" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg/255px-Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg/340px-Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1225" data-file-height="1914" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mickey_Mantle_1953.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a> was one of the franchise\'s most celebrated hitters, highlighted by his 1956 Triple Crown.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Arnold_Johnson_(industrialist)" title="Arnold Johnson (industrialist)">Arnold Johnson</a>, owner of the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansas City Athletics">Kansas City Athletics</a>, was a longtime business associate of then-Yankees co-owners <a href="/wiki/Del_Webb" title="Del Webb">Del Webb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dan_Topping" title="Dan Topping">Dan Topping</a>. Because of this "special relationship" with the Yankees, he traded young players to them in exchange for cash and aging veterans. Invariably, these trades ended up being heavily tilted in the Yankees\' favor, leading to accusations that the Athletics were little more than a Yankee farm team at the major league level.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Kansas City had been home to the Yankees\' top farm team for almost 20 years before the Athletics moved there from Philadelphia in 1954.\n</p><p>In 1960, <a href="/wiki/Charles_O._Finley" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles O. Finley">Charles O. Finley</a> purchased the Athletics and put an end to the trades. But the Yankees had already strengthened their supply of future prospects, which included a young outfielder named <a href="/wiki/Roger_Maris" title="Roger Maris">Roger Maris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 1960, Maris led the league in slugging percentage, RBIs, and extra base hits. He finished second in home runs (one behind Mantle) and total bases, and won a <a href="/wiki/Gold_Glove" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold Glove">Gold Glove</a>, which garnered enough votes for the American League MVP award.\n</p><p>The year 1961 was one of the most memorable in Yankee history. Throughout the summer, Mantle and Maris hit home runs at a fast pace, and became known as the "M&amp;M Boys".<sup id="cite_ref-Secyclo_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secyclo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, a severe hip infection forced Mantle to leave the lineup and drop out of the race. Maris continued though, and on October 1 (the last day of the regular season), he hit home run number 61, surpassing Babe Ruth\'s single season home run record of 60.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> However, MLB Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Ford_Frick" title="Ford Frick">Ford Frick</a> (who, as it was discovered later, had <a href="/wiki/Ghostwriter" title="Ghostwriter">ghostwritten</a> for Babe Ruth during his career) decreed that since Maris had played in a 162-game season, and Ruth (in 1927) had played in a 154-game season, two separate records would be kept. It was 30 years before the dual record was done away with, and Maris held the record alone until <a href="/wiki/Mark_McGwire" title="Mark McGwire">Mark McGwire</a> broke it in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Maris still holds the American League record.\n</p><p>The Yankees won the pennant with a 109\xe2\x80\x9353 record and went on to defeat the <a href="/wiki/1961_Cincinnati_Reds_season" title="1961 Cincinnati Reds season">Cincinnati Reds</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1961_World_Series" title="1961 World Series">1961 World Series</a>. The team finished the year with a record 240 home runs.\n</p><p>After the 1957 departures of the Dodgers and Giants for the West Coast, New York City was a one-team town for the first time since professional play began. In 1962, the sports scene in New York changed when the National League expanded to include a new expansion team, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a>, who played at the Giants\' former home, the <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a>, for two seasons while <a href="/wiki/Shea_Stadium" title="Shea Stadium">Shea Stadium</a> was under construction in nearby <a href="/wiki/Flushing,_Queens" title="Flushing, Queens">Flushing, Queens</a>. The <a href="/wiki/1962_New_York_Mets_season" title="1962 New York Mets season">Mets</a> lost a record 120 games while the <a href="/wiki/1962_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1962 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> won the <a href="/wiki/1962_World_Series" title="1962 World Series">1962 World Series</a>, their tenth in the past sixteen years, defeating the <a href="/wiki/1962_San_Francisco_Giants_season" title="1962 San Francisco Giants season">San Francisco Giants</a> in seven games. It was the Yankees\' last championship until <a href="/wiki/1977_World_Series" title="1977 World Series">1977</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1963_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1963 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> reached the <a href="/wiki/1963_World_Series" title="1963 World Series">1963 World Series</a>, but were swept by the <a href="/wiki/1963_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1963 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> and their ace pitcher <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Koufax" title="Sandy Koufax">Sandy Koufax</a>.\n</p><p>After the season, Yogi Berra, who had just retired from playing, took over managerial duties. The aging <a href="/wiki/1964_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1964 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> returned the next year for <a href="/wiki/1964_World_Series" title="1964 World Series">a fifth straight World Series</a>, but were beaten in seven games by the <a href="/wiki/1964_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1964 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis Cardinals</a>. It was the Yankees\' last World Series appearance until 1976, when they were swept by the <a href="/wiki/Big_Red_Machine" title="Big Red Machine">Big Red Machine</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="New_ownership_and_a_steep_decline_.281965.E2.80.931972.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_ownership_and_a_steep_decline_(1965\xe2\x80\x931972)">New ownership and a steep decline (1965\xe2\x80\x931972)</span></h3>\n<p>After the 1964 season, <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> purchased 80% of the Yankees from Topping and Webb for $11.2 million.<sup id="cite_ref-1951to1975_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1951to1975-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> With the new ownership, the team began to decline. In fact, the Yankees finished in the <a href="/wiki/Second_division_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="Second division (baseball)">second division</a> in 1965\xe2\x80\x94their first losing record in 40 years, and only their second in 47 years.\n</p><p>In 1966, the <a href="/wiki/1966_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1966 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> finished last in the AL for the first time since 1912.  It also marked their first consecutive losing seasons since 1917 and 1918.  They finished next-to-last in <a href="/wiki/1967_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1967 New York Yankees season">1967</a>.  While their fortunes improved somewhat in the late 1960s and early 1970s, they only finished higher than fourth once during CBS\' ownership, in 1970.  Topping and Webb had owned the Yankees for 20 years, missing the World Series only five times and going 10\xe2\x80\x935 in the ones they did get to. By contrast, the CBS-owned teams never went to the World Series.\n</p><p>Various reasons<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2017)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> have been given<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (August 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> for the decline, but the single biggest one was the Yankees\' inability to replace their aging superstars with promising young talent, as they had consistently done in the previous five decades.  As early as the 1961-62 off-season, longtime fans noticed that the pipeline of talent had started to dry up.  This was worsened<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Please clarify the following statement or statements with a good explanation from a reliable source. (August 2017)">how?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> by the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Draft" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Draft">major league amateur draft</a> that year, which meant that the Yankees could no longer sign any player they wanted.  While the Yankees usually drafted fairly early during this period due to their lackluster records, <a href="/wiki/Thurman_Munson" title="Thurman Munson">Thurman Munson</a> was the only pick who lived up to his billing.\n</p><p>At the start of this period, all-time "Voice of the Yankees" <a href="/wiki/Mel_Allen" title="Mel Allen">Mel Allen</a>, the team\'s top announcer since 1939, was fired after the 1964 season, supposedly due to cost-cutting measures by longtime broadcast sponsor <a href="/wiki/Ballantine_(brewery)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballantine (brewery)">Ballantine Beer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VoicesBook_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VoicesBook-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During baseball\'s centennial season in 1969, the greatest players at each position for every team were named during a voting. The all-time Yankees were: <a href="/wiki/Bill_Dickey" title="Bill Dickey">Bill Dickey</a> (catcher), <a href="/wiki/Whitey_Ford" title="Whitey Ford">Whitey Ford</a> (left-handed pitcher), <a href="/wiki/Red_Ruffing" title="Red Ruffing">Red Ruffing</a> (right-handed pitcher), <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Murphy" title="Johnny Murphy">Johnny Murphy</a> (relief pitcher),  <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a> (first base), <a href="/wiki/Tony_Lazzeri" title="Tony Lazzeri">Tony Lazzeri</a> (second base), <a href="/wiki/Phil_Rizzuto" title="Phil Rizzuto">Phil Rizzuto</a> (shortstop), <a href="/wiki/Red_Rolfe" title="Red Rolfe">Red Rolfe</a> (third base), <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a> (center field, also named baseball\'s "Greatest Living Player"), <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> (right field), <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a> (left field), and <a href="/wiki/Casey_Stengel" title="Casey Stengel">Casey Stengel</a> (manager).\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Steinbrenner.2C_Martin.2C_Jackson.2C_and_Munson:_the_Bronx_Zoo_.281973.E2.80.931981.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Steinbrenner,_Martin,_Jackson,_and_Munson:_the_Bronx_Zoo_(1973\xe2\x80\x931981)">Steinbrenner, Martin, Jackson, and Munson: the Bronx Zoo (1973\xe2\x80\x931981)</span></h3>\n<p>A group of investors, led by <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>-based shipbuilder <a href="/wiki/George_Steinbrenner" title="George Steinbrenner">George Steinbrenner</a> (1930\xe2\x80\x932010), purchased the club from CBS on January 3, 1973, for $8.7 million.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Mike Burke stayed on as president until he quit in April. Within a year, Steinbrenner bought out most of his other partners and became the team\'s principal owner, although Burke continued to hold a minority share into the 1980s.\n</p><p>One of Steinbrenner\'s major goals was to renovate Yankee Stadium.  It had greatly deteriorated by the late 1960s, and the surrounding neighborhood had gone south as well. CBS initially suggested renovations, but the team needed to play elsewhere, and the Mets refused to open their home, <a href="/wiki/Shea_Stadium" title="Shea Stadium">Shea Stadium</a>, to the Yankees. A new stadium in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Meadowlands" title="New Jersey Meadowlands">the Meadowlands</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, was suggested (and was eventually built, as <a href="/wiki/Giants_Stadium" title="Giants Stadium">Giants Stadium</a>, specifically for football). Finally, in mid-1972, Mayor <a href="/wiki/John_Lindsay" title="John Lindsay">John Lindsay</a> stepped in. The city bought the stadium and began an extensive two-year renovation period. Since the city owned Shea, the Mets had to allow the Yankees to play two seasons there. The renovations modernized the look of the stadium, significantly altered the dimensions, and reconfigured some of the seating.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg/220px-Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg/330px-Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg/440px-Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1952" data-file-height="1480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yankee_Stadium_aerial_from_Blackhawk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>During 1974 and 1975, <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a> was renovated into its final shape and structure, as shown here in 2002, seven years before demolition.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the 1974 <a href="/wiki/1974_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1974 Major League Baseball season">season</a>, Steinbrenner made a move that started the modern era of <a href="/wiki/Free_agent" title="Free agent">free agency</a>, signing star pitcher <a href="/wiki/Catfish_Hunter" title="Catfish Hunter">Catfish Hunter</a> away from Oakland. Midway through the 1975 <a href="/wiki/1975_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1975 Major League Baseball season">season</a>, Steinbrenner made another move, hiring former second baseman <a href="/wiki/Billy_Martin" title="Billy Martin">Billy Martin</a> as manager. With Martin at the helm, the <a href="/wiki/1976_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1976 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> reached the <a href="/wiki/1976_World_Series" title="1976 World Series">1976 World Series</a>, but were swept by the <a href="/wiki/1976_Cincinnati_Reds_season" title="1976 Cincinnati Reds season">Cincinnati Reds</a> and their famed "<a href="/wiki/Big_Red_Machine" title="Big Red Machine">Big Red Machine</a>."\n</p><p>After the 1976 campaign, Steinbrenner added star Oakland outfielder <a href="/wiki/Reggie_Jackson" title="Reggie Jackson">Reggie Jackson</a>\xe2\x80\x94who had spent 1976 with the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a>\xe2\x80\x94to his roster. During spring training of 1977, Jackson alienated his teammates with controversial remarks about the Yankees captain, catcher <a href="/wiki/Thurman_Munson" title="Thurman Munson">Thurman Munson</a>. He had bad blood with manager Billy Martin, who had managed the <a href="/wiki/1972_Detroit_Tigers_season" title="1972 Detroit Tigers season">Detroit Tigers</a> when Jackson\'s <a href="/wiki/1972_Oakland_Athletics_season" title="1972 Oakland Athletics season">Athletics</a> defeated them in the <a href="/wiki/1972_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1972 American League Championship Series">1972 playoffs</a>. Jackson, Martin, and Steinbrenner repeatedly feuded with each other throughout Jackson\'s 5-year contract. Martin was hired and fired by Steinbrenner five times over the next 13 years. This conflict, combined with the extremely rowdy Yankees fans of the late 1970s and the bad conditions of the Bronx, led to the Yankee organization and stadium being referred to as the "Bronx Zoo."<sup id="cite_ref-BronxZoo_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronxZoo-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Despite the turmoil, Jackson starred in the <a href="/wiki/1977_World_Series" title="1977 World Series">1977 World Series</a>, when he hit three home runs in the same game, and overall, four home runs on four consecutive pitches from four different pitchers. Jackson\'s great performance in the postseason earned him the <a href="/wiki/World_Series_MVP_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="World Series MVP Award">Series MVP Award</a>, and the nickname "Mr. October."<sup id="cite_ref-MrOctober_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MrOctober-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout the late 1970s, the race for the pennant was often a close competition between the Yankees and the Red Sox. In the 1950s and early 1960s, the Yankees had been dominant while the Red Sox were largely a non-factor. However, in the late 1960s and early 1970s, the Yankees were mired in second place and the Red Sox led the league. In the late 1970s the two teams were contending simultaneously and locked in a close fight.\n</p><p>On July 14, 1978, the <a href="/wiki/1978_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1978 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> were &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">14<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> games behind the <a href="/wiki/1978_Boston_Red_Sox_season" title="1978 Boston Red Sox season">Red Sox</a>. In late July, Martin suspended Reggie Jackson for "defiance" after he bunted while Martin had the "swing" signal on.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Upon Jackson\'s return, Martin made a famous statement against both Jackson and owner Steinbrenner: "They deserve each other. One\'s a born liar; the other\'s convicted." Martin was forced to resign the next day and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Lemon" title="Bob Lemon">Bob Lemon</a>. This came while the team was winning five games in a row and Boston was losing five in a row.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Yankees continued to win games, making up ground and by the time they met Boston for a pivotal four-game series at <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a> in early September, they were only four games behind the Red Sox. The Yankees swept the Red Sox in what became known as the "Boston Massacre", winning the games 15\xe2\x80\x933, 13\xe2\x80\x932, 7\xe2\x80\x930, and 7\xe2\x80\x934. The third game was a shutout pitched by "Louisiana Lightning" <a href="/wiki/Ron_Guidry" title="Ron Guidry">Ron Guidry</a>, who led the majors with nine shutouts, a 25\xe2\x80\x933 record, and a 1.74 ERA. Guidry finished with 248 strikeouts, but <a href="/wiki/Nolan_Ryan" title="Nolan Ryan">Nolan Ryan</a>\'s 260 strikeouts with the <a href="/wiki/California_Angels" class="mw-redirect" title="California Angels">California Angels</a> deprived Guidry of the pitching <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Triple_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Triple Crown">Triple Crown</a>.\n</p><p>On the last day of the season, the two clubs finished in a tie for first place in the AL East, and a one-game playoff (the 163rd game of the regular season) was held at Fenway Park. With Guidry pitching against former Yankee <a href="/wiki/Mike_Torrez" title="Mike Torrez">Mike Torrez</a>, the Red Sox took an early 2\xe2\x80\x930 lead. In the seventh inning, light-hitting Yankee shortstop <a href="/wiki/Bucky_Dent" title="Bucky Dent">Bucky Dent</a> drove a three-run home run over the "<a href="/wiki/Green_Monster" title="Green Monster">Green Monster</a>" (Fenway Park\'s famed left field wall), putting the Yankees up 3\xe2\x80\x932. Reggie Jackson\'s solo home run in the following inning sealed the eventual 5\xe2\x80\x934 win that gave the Yankees their one hundredth win of the season and their third straight AL East title and Guidry earned his 25th win of the season.\n</p><p>After beating the <a href="/wiki/1978_Kansas_City_Royals_season" title="1978 Kansas City Royals season">Kansas City Royals</a> for the third consecutive year in the <a href="/wiki/1978_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1978 American League Championship Series">ALCS</a>, the Yankees faced the <a href="/wiki/1978_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1978 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Dodgers</a> again in the <a href="/wiki/1978_World_Series" title="1978 World Series">World Series</a>. They lost the first two games in LA, but won all three games at Yankee Stadium and won Game 6 in Los Angeles, winning their 22nd World Championship.\n</p><p>Changes occurred during the 1979 season. Former Cy Young Award-winning closer <a href="/wiki/Sparky_Lyle" title="Sparky Lyle">Sparky Lyle</a> was traded to the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> for several players, including <a href="/wiki/Dave_Righetti" title="Dave Righetti">Dave Righetti</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tommy_John" title="Tommy John">Tommy John</a> was acquired from the Dodgers and <a href="/wiki/Luis_Tiant" title="Luis Tiant">Luis Tiant</a> from the hated Red Sox to bolster the pitching staff. During the season, Bob Lemon was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Billy_Martin" title="Billy Martin">Billy Martin</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Munson_Gear.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Munson_Gear.jpg/220px-Munson_Gear.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Munson_Gear.jpg/330px-Munson_Gear.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Munson_Gear.jpg/440px-Munson_Gear.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2856" data-file-height="2142" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Munson_Gear.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mask and catcher\'s mitt of <a href="/wiki/Thurman_Munson" title="Thurman Munson">Thurman Munson</a>, the team captain who was killed in a plane crash in 1979</div></div></div>\n<p>The 1970s ended on a tragic note for the Yankees. On August 2, 1979, <a href="/wiki/Thurman_Munson" title="Thurman Munson">Thurman Munson</a> died after crashing his private plane while practicing "Touch and Go" landings.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Four days later, the entire team flew out to <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Ohio" title="Canton, Ohio">Canton</a>, Ohio, for the funeral, despite having a game later that day against the <a href="/wiki/1979_Baltimore_Orioles_season" title="1979 Baltimore Orioles season">Orioles</a>. Martin adamantly stated that the funeral was more important, and that he did not care if they made it back in time. <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Murcer" title="Bobby Murcer">Bobby Murcer</a>, a close friend of Munson\'s, was chosen to give the eulogy at his funeral. In a nationally televised and emotional game, Murcer used Munson\'s bat (which he gave to his fallen friend\'s wife after the game), and drove in all five of the team\'s runs in a dramatic 5\xe2\x80\x934 walk-off victory. Before the game, Munson\'s locker sat empty except for his catching gear, a sad reminder for his teammates. His locker, labeled with his number 15, has remained empty in the Yankee clubhouse as a memorial. When the Yankees moved across the street, Munson\'s locker was torn out and installed in the new Stadium\'s museum. The number 15 has been retired by the team.\n</p><p>The 1980 season brought more changes to the Yankees. Billy Martin was fired once again and <a href="/wiki/Dick_Howser" title="Dick Howser">Dick Howser</a> took his place. <a href="/wiki/Chris_Chambliss" title="Chris Chambliss">Chris Chambliss</a> was traded to the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Blue_Jays" title="Toronto Blue Jays">Toronto Blue Jays</a> for catcher <a href="/wiki/Rick_Cerone" title="Rick Cerone">Rick Cerone</a>. Thanks to Howser\'s no-nonsense attitude, Reggie Jackson hit .300 for the only time in his career with 41 homers, and finished 2nd in the MVP voting to Kansas City\'s <a href="/wiki/George_Brett" title="George Brett">George Brett</a>. The <a href="/wiki/1980_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1980 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> won 103 games and the AL East by three games over the 100-win <a href="/wiki/1980_Baltimore_Orioles_season" title="1980 Baltimore Orioles season">Baltimore Orioles</a>, but were swept by the <a href="/wiki/1980_Kansas_City_Royals_season" title="1980 Kansas City Royals season">Royals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1980_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1980 American League Championship Series">1980 ALCS</a>.\n</p><p>After the season ended, the Yankees signed <a href="/wiki/Dave_Winfield" title="Dave Winfield">Dave Winfield</a> to a 10-year contract. The Yankees fired Howser and replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Gene_Michael" title="Gene Michael">Gene Michael</a>. Under Michael, the Yankees led the AL East before a <a href="/wiki/1981_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1981 Major League Baseball strike">strike</a> hit in June 1981. In the second half of the season, the Yankees struggled under <a href="/wiki/Bob_Lemon" title="Bob Lemon">Bob Lemon</a>, who replaced Michael. Thanks to the split-season playoff format, the Yankees faced the second-half winner <a href="/wiki/1981_Milwaukee_Brewers_season" title="1981 Milwaukee Brewers season">Milwaukee Brewers</a> in the special <a href="/wiki/1981_American_League_Division_Series" title="1981 American League Division Series">1981 American League Division Series</a>. After narrowly defeating Milwaukee in five games, they breezed through <a href="/wiki/Billy_Martin" title="Billy Martin">Billy Martin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1981_Oakland_Athletics_season" title="1981 Oakland Athletics season">Oakland Athletics</a> in a three-game <a href="/wiki/1981_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1981 American League Championship Series">ALCS</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/1981_World_Series" title="1981 World Series">World Series</a>, the Yankees got off to a hot start by winning the first two games against the <a href="/wiki/1981_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1981 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>. But the Dodgers fought back and stunned the Yankees by winning the next four games to clinch their first World Series title since <a href="/wiki/1965_World_Series" title="1965 World Series">1965</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Struggles:_The_Mattingly_years_.281982.E2.80.931995.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Struggles:_The_Mattingly_years_(1982\xe2\x80\x931995)">Struggles: The Mattingly years (1982\xe2\x80\x931995)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg/170px-Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg/255px-Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg/340px-Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1135" data-file-height="1799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Don_Mattingly_Strikes_Out.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Don_Mattingly" title="Don Mattingly">Don Mattingly</a> headlined a Yankees franchise that struggled in the 1980s.</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the team\'s loss to the Dodgers in the 1981 World Series, the Yankees began their longest absence from the playoffs since 1921. Following the example set by the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Big_Red_Machine" title="Big Red Machine">Big Red Machine</a> that had defeated his team in the <a href="/wiki/1976_World_Series" title="1976 World Series">1976 World Series</a>, George Steinbrenner announced his plan to transform the Yankees from the Bronx Bombers into the "Bronx Burners", increasing the Yankees\' ability to win games based on speed and defense<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> instead of "waiting around for a 3 run home run". As a first step towards this end, the Yankees signed <a href="/wiki/Dave_Collins" title="Dave Collins">Dave Collins</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Reds" title="Cincinnati Reds">Cincinnati Reds</a> during the 1981 off-season.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Collins was traded to the Toronto Blue Jays after the 1982 season in a deal that also included future All-Stars <a href="/wiki/Fred_McGriff" title="Fred McGriff">Fred McGriff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mike_Morgan_(baseball)" title="Mike Morgan (baseball)">Mike Morgan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In return the Yankees got <a href="/wiki/Dale_Murray" title="Dale Murray">Dale Murray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Dodd" title="Tom Dodd">Tom Dodd</a>.\n</p><p>The Yankees of the 1980s were led by All-Star first baseman <a href="/wiki/Don_Mattingly" title="Don Mattingly">Don Mattingly</a>.  In spite of accumulating the most total wins of any major league team, they failed to win a World Series (the first such Yankees team since the 1910s) and had only 1 playoff appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Although they consistently had a powerful offense\xe2\x80\x94Mattingly at various times was teammate to <a href="/wiki/Dave_Winfield" title="Dave Winfield">Dave Winfield</a> (whom Mattingly battled for the AL batting title throughout most of the 1984 season), <a href="/wiki/Rickey_Henderson" title="Rickey Henderson">Rickey Henderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Don_Baylor" title="Don Baylor">Don Baylor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Griffey,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Ken Griffey, Sr.">Ken Griffey, Sr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pagliarulo" title="Mike Pagliarulo">Mike Pagliarulo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Sax" title="Steve Sax">Steve Sax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Barfield" title="Jesse Barfield">Jesse Barfield</a>, and the Yankees led the majors in runs scored for the decade\xe2\x80\x94the Yankees teams of the 1980s lacked sufficient starting pitching to win a championship. After posting a 22\xe2\x80\x936 record in 1985, arm problems caught up with Ron Guidry, and his performance declined over the next three years. Of the remaining mainstays of the Yankees\' rotation, only <a href="/wiki/Dave_Righetti" title="Dave Righetti">Dave Righetti</a> stood out, pitching a <a href="/wiki/No-hitter" title="No-hitter">no-hitter</a> on July 4, 1983, but he was moved to the bullpen the next year where he helped to define the closer role. Despite the Yankees\' lack of pitching success during the 1980s, they had three of the premier pitchers of the early 1990s on their roster during these years in <a href="/wiki/Al_Leiter" title="Al Leiter">Al Leiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doug_Drabek" title="Doug Drabek">Doug Drabek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Rijo" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Rijo">Jos\xc3\xa9 Rijo</a>. All were mismanaged and dealt away before they could reach their full potential, with only Rijo returning much value \xe2\x80\x93 he was traded to the Oakland A\'s in the deal that brought Henderson to New York.\n</p><p>The team came close to winning the AL East in <a href="/wiki/1985_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1985 New York Yankees season">1985</a> and <a href="/wiki/1986_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1986 New York Yankees season">1986</a>, finishing second to the <a href="/wiki/1985_Toronto_Blue_Jays_season" title="1985 Toronto Blue Jays season">Toronto Blue Jays</a> and <a href="/wiki/1986_Boston_Red_Sox_season" title="1986 Boston Red Sox season">Boston Red Sox</a> (who lost in the <a href="/wiki/1986_World_Series" title="1986 World Series">World Series</a> that year to the Yankees\' <a href="/wiki/Mets-Yankees_rivalry" class="mw-redirect" title="Mets-Yankees rivalry">cross-town rivals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a>) respectively, but fell to fourth place in <a href="/wiki/1987_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1987 New York Yankees season">1987</a> and fifth in <a href="/wiki/1988_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1988 New York Yankees season">1988</a>, despite having mid-season leads in the AL East standings both years. Despite their lack of championships and playoff appearances the Yankees posted the highest winning percentage of all MLB teams during the 1980s.\n</p><p>By the end of the decade, the Yankees\' offense was on the decline. Henderson and Pagliarulo had departed by the middle of <a href="/wiki/1989_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1989 New York Yankees season">1989</a>, while back problems hampered both Winfield (who missed the entire \'89 season) and Mattingly (who missed almost the entire second half of 1990). Winfield\'s tenure with the team ended when he was dealt to the Angels. From 1989 to 1992, the team had a losing record, spending significant money on free-agents and draft picks who did not live up to expectations. In 1990, the <a href="/wiki/1990_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1990 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> had the worst record in the American League, and their fourth last-place finish in franchise history.\n</p><p>On July 1, 1990, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Andy_Hawkins" title="Andy Hawkins">Andy Hawkins</a> became the first Yankee ever to lose despite throwing a no-hitter. Third baseman <a href="/wiki/Mike_Blowers" title="Mike Blowers">Mike Blowers</a> committed an error, followed by two walks and an error by the left fielder <a href="/wiki/Jim_Leyritz" title="Jim Leyritz">Jim Leyritz</a> with the bases loaded, scoring all three runners and the batter. The 4\xe2\x80\x930 loss to the <a href="/wiki/1990_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1990 Chicago White Sox season">Chicago White Sox</a> was the largest margin of any no-hitter loss in the 20th century. Ironically, the Yankees (and Hawkins) were no-hit for six innings in a rain-shortened game against the White Sox 11 days later.\n</p><p>During the 1990 season, Yankee fans started to chant "1918!" to taunt the Red Sox, reminding them of the last time they won a World Series one weekend the Red Sox were there in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Each time the Red Sox were at Yankee Stadium afterward, demeaning chants of "1918!" echoed through the stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-1918!Chants_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1918!Chants-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Yankee fans also taunted the Red Sox with signs saying "CURSE OF THE BAMBINO", pictures of Babe Ruth, and wearing "1918!" T-shirts each time they were at the stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-1918!Chants_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1918!Chants-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The poor showings in the 1980s and 1990s soon changed. Steinbrenner hired Howard Spira to uncover damaging information on Winfield and was subsequently suspended from day-to-day team operations by Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Fay_Vincent" title="Fay Vincent">Fay Vincent</a> when the plot was revealed. This turn of events allowed management to implement a coherent acquisition/development program without owner interference. General Manager <a href="/wiki/Gene_Michael" title="Gene Michael">Gene Michael</a>, along with manager <a href="/wiki/Buck_Showalter" title="Buck Showalter">Buck Showalter</a>, shifted the club\'s emphasis from high-priced acquisitions to developing talent through the <a href="/wiki/Farm_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Farm system">farm system</a>. This new philosophy developed key players such as outfielder <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Williams" title="Bernie Williams">Bernie Williams</a>, shortstop <a href="/wiki/Derek_Jeter" title="Derek Jeter">Derek Jeter</a>, catcher <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Posada" title="Jorge Posada">Jorge Posada</a>, and pitchers <a href="/wiki/Andy_Pettitte" title="Andy Pettitte">Andy Pettitte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mariano_Rivera" title="Mariano Rivera">Mariano Rivera</a>. The first significant success came in <a href="/wiki/1994_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1994 New York Yankees season">1994</a>, when the Yankees had the best record in the AL, but the <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1994\xe2\x80\x9395 Major League Baseball strike">strike</a> ended the season and Mattingly\'s best chance for a World Series title and is remembered among the 10 worst moments in New York City sports history.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Because the Yankees were last in a postseason in a season cut short by a <a href="/wiki/1981_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1981 Major League Baseball strike">strike</a>, the news media constantly reminded the Yankees about the parallels between the two Yankee teams (1981 and 1994), which included both Yankee teams having division leads taken away by strike.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Throughout October, the media continued to speculate about what might have been if there had not been a strike, making references to the day\'s games in the post-season would have been played.<sup id="cite_ref-LostWorldSeries_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LostWorldSeries-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A year later, the team qualified for the playoffs in the new wild card slot in the strike shortened 1995 season. In the memorable <a href="/wiki/1995_American_League_Division_Series" title="1995 American League Division Series">1995 American League Division Series</a> against the <a href="/wiki/1995_Seattle_Mariners_season" title="1995 Seattle Mariners season">Seattle Mariners</a>, the Yankees won the first two games at home and dropped the next three in Seattle. Mattingly, suffering greatly from his back injury, retired after the 1995 season. He had the unfortunate distinction of beginning and ending his career on years bookended by Yankee World Series appearances (1981 and 1996).\n</p>\n<h3><span id="New_dynasty_.281996.E2.80.932007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_dynasty_(1996\xe2\x80\x932007)">New dynasty (1996\xe2\x80\x932007)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Key_Three.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Key_Three.jpg/220px-Key_Three.jpg" width="220" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Key_Three.jpg/330px-Key_Three.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Key_Three.jpg/440px-Key_Three.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2119" data-file-height="2242" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a
